It is not clear to me that such an example exists, for
the computer; but the inversion may not be as clear to
people; and may, though I can not point to a source,
fail analysis by Formal Methods.

> (a < b) is no different from (a >= b) and both are expressed in
> "positive logic".  (A silly qualifier -- even negative logic is logic.

> logic.)

the context of an actual program. It is this perception
of a difference that makes simple inversion obscure
and leads to "obfuscation".

> Even (counter-limit > counter) would make a decent test for your example
> as you would obviously enter exception processing for the first case of
> (counter-limit = counter) and would not process a subsequent iteration.

It would be an equivalent test to the original but would
not necessarily be perceived as such, as readily. It would
also use 'continue' to which you object as "obfuscation".
```

```

"Joe Zitzelberger" <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote in message
news:joe_zitzelberger-930104.21124008112005@ispnews.usenetserver.com...
> In article <11mkv8gossdh2f2@corp.supernews.com>,
>  "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote:
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> > > Those that complain about it seem to be unable to understand any form
of
> > > logic.)
> >
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> "(a >=b)" is not qualified to charge for their services.

Then, those who refuse to use such formulations when
such use results in loss of clarity are ... what? More
qualified? Should charge more?

> People tend to forget that we are in the business of twitching bits, a
> "programmer" that does not understand this basic fact cannot understand
> most of what they are writing.  As such, they have no point selling
> themselves as "programmers".

So, those who do not agree with "this basic fact" and
provide solutions written with clearly stated conditions
"cannot understand most of what they are writing"?

> Inherent in true understanding of any logic concept, such as "A<B", is
> understanding its complement, regardless if it is expressed "B>=A" or
> "NOT (A<B)".

H'm, "B>=A"? A strange way to make a point! <g>

I think, overall, your "value judgments" are showing!

While I agree that there may be different ways to express an
understanding of a complement, it is only "not (a < b)" that can
be the complement of the relation "a < b". [Both "not <" and
">=" may be complements to the relational operator "<"; but
this is not the same as complement (or negation) in Boolean
Algebra, at least as I understand it.]

Now consider,

    evaluate true
      when a < b
        *> acceptable range
      when a = b
        *> acceptable, but at limit
      when a > b
        *> unacceptable, condition can not occur, guards in place
    end-evaluate

If "a > b" can not occur, then, logically, "not (a < b)" might only
be "a = b". It is not Boolean Algebra, but it is practicable
without being illogical.

I am going to omit most of a quote that concludes, "The
expression of ordinary language that captures the logical
sense of negation is 'It is not the case that ...'."

To assume that "not (a < b)" must be "a >= b", incorrectly
assumes that both "a > b" and "a = b"  may be true, at
different times; when it is only logical to state, it is not the
case that "a < b", or only "not (a < b)".

Here follows something that defies "logic". The two programs
are functionally identical but provide different results. This is
an example pitting theory against practice.

-----COBOL program

      $set ans85 flag"ans85" flagas"s"
       identification division.
       program-id. a-less-b.
       data division.
       working-storage section.
       1 b comp pic 9(4) value 0.
       1 a comp pic s9(4) value -1.
       procedure division.
       begin.
           if a < b
               display "A is less than B"
           else
               display "It is not true that A is less than B"
           end-if
           stop run.
       end program a-less-b.

-----Output from COBOL program

A is less than B

-----C++ program

#include <cstdio>

unsigned b=0;
int a=-1;
int main (void)
{
    if (a < b)
        printf ("%s\n", "A is less than B");
    else
        printf ("%s\n", "It is not true that A is less than B");
    return 0;
}

-----Output from C++ program

It is not true that A is less than B
Press any key to continue
-----

The C++ compiler did produce a warning for an
signed/unsigned mismatch. When changed to
if (a < (int) b), it worked as expected. But, it is practice
and not theory that clarified the condition.
```

"Joe Zitzelberger" <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> schrieb im Newsbeitrag 
news:joe_zitzelberger-930104.21124008112005@ispnews.usenetserver.com...
> A programmer that cannot tell the difference between "not (a < b)" and

My experience is, that there are programmers, that take pride in obscuring 
things. For me the issue is, that a progranm is only written once, but read 
far more often.
Now such a simple negated condition taken alone, really should not be much 
of a hurdle, but still it remains a hurdle, however small it might be. When 
I read and try to understand a programm, it is the interaction of a lot of 
such small hurdles, that make things complex and difficult. Most have 
already been discussed here, just to repeat some:
- negated conditions
- inadequate or misleading datanames (especially 'negated' datanames eg for 
switches)
- sparse comments (especially undocumented semantics/valid ranges of certain 
values)
- and always the vague suspicion, that the code I am looking at might 
contain errors, not yet detected.
For me the problem is not only 'understanding, what other programmers have 
written', but also how much time it takes! I believe, that doing away with 
as much of these small hurdles as possible, will speed up this understanding 
a lot. If desired, next week I could provide for illustration some 40 lines 
of code - not COBOL, but only C - which I came across just recently.

regards   K. Kiesel

In article <dl1o4n$7as$1@nntp.fujitsu-siemens.com>, "Karl Kiesel" <Karl.Kiesel@fujitsu-siemens.com> writes:
> 
> "Joe Zitzelberger" <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> schrieb im Newsbeitrag 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> things.

I think that's a basic inclination shared by many people, not only
programmers.  Performing a task in an obscure, stylized, or
convoluted fashion seems to tickle some innate desire to be creative
and clever.  Obfuscated code is not really so different from, say,
building a ship in a bottle.

Some programmers are just more prone to succumbing to that urge.

> For me the issue is, that a progranm is only written once, but read 
> far more often.

I'm sure we've all seen statistics about the relative cost of
maintenance versus initial development, and in general I agree with
you: code should be written so that it's easy for a maintainer to
understand, within reason.

However, I also take Joe's point - we should demand a certain level
of competence from programmers, who are after all supposed to be
skilled workers.

And, of course, there are cases where convoluted or inobvious code
is called for due to constraints like performance requirements.

So in my opinion we need to temper demands for code clarity - though
generally a good thing - with on the one hand the recognition that
this can be carried further than is reasonable, and on the other
that there are times when it has to yield to more pressing concerns.

In the case of this specific example, I don't personally object to
either form, but I'd have to see it in context to decide if I thought
one variant or another was preferable.
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<Go5df.13479$Dk.12818@bignews5.bellsouth.net>`
- **References:** `<9OV3f.23230$S4.19969@edtnps84> <joe_zitzelberger-58F137.22362830102005@ispnews.usenetserver.com> <jqq9f.17714$wG.5325@bignews4.bellsouth.net> <11mda4srum62u56@corp.supernews.com> <joe_zitzelberger-C14A4C.09040301112005@ispnews.usenetserver.com> <11mfh6f8g64hb80@corp.supernews.com> <joe_zitzelberger-849C45.04113503112005@ispnews.usenetserver.com> <11mkv8gossdh2f2@corp.supernews.com> <joe_zitzelberger-930104.21124008112005@ispnews.usenetserver.com> <dl1o4n$7as$1@nntp.fujitsu-siemens.com> <dl2j7l09u5@news2.newsguy.com>`

```
"Michael Wojcik" <mwojcik@newsguy.com> wrote:
>
> However, I also take Joe's point - we should demand a certain level
> of competence from programmers, who are after all supposed to be
> skilled workers.

I don't disagree, but that statement implies enough competence in 
mananagement to implement the demand, and that's problematic. :-)

> And, of course, there are cases where convoluted or inobvious code
> is called for due to constraints like performance requirements.

Years ago I was asked by a client to rewrite a program (actually, two 
similar programs) that produced a number of interrelated reports. There were 
several design goals. Particularly, they wanted the program to be flexible 
enough to allow varying numbers of (data driven) vertical columns across the 
page, even to automatically overflowing onto extra passes of the report that 
could be placed side by side so as to match from end to end, even when 
subsequent passes had interspersed blank pages, and they wanted little or no 
maintenance required. After a bit of analysis I reported to management that 
there were two ways I could go. One way was to keep the report as simple as 
possible, though it would still be pretty complex, and it would require code 
changes in maintenance. The other way was to write a very sophisticated and 
flexible table driven report section, so the programmer would rarely 
(hopefully never) have to modify that code, but would only need to update 
the report descriptor table. The IS director wanted the latter approach. The 
code was highly complex and it took me two weeks to write and debug the 
report driver section. I placed dire warning comments about modifying the 
report driver, with detailed instructions on how to modify the table. 
Several years later a subsequent IS director called to chew me out about 
writing such a difficult to modify program. Turns out, one of his 
programmers had jumped in and started trying to modify the report driver 
without bothering to read my comments. She quickly realized she was in way 
over her head and cried "Foul!" Once I explained, he was okay with it, and 
the programmer was instructed to read my comments and modify the table. 
Worked fine, and that's the only response I've had in the almost 15 years 
the program has been running. Had we used a less sophisticated approach, the 
actual code would have necessarily been modified a number of times by now, 
and even a simplified, non-table driven version would be pretty rough going 
for the average programmer.
```

```
Karl Kiesel wrote:
> "Joe Zitzelberger" <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> schrieb im Newsbeitrag 
> news:joe_zitzelberger-930104.21124008112005@ispnews.usenetserver.com...
…[32 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
Karl,

Want to get rid of a hurdle ?  Introduce to your Fujitsu-Siemens 
compiler the Micro Focus technique, 'Cut out the red tape crap'. 
Ignoring the comment lines which are my style for clarity, the following 
is a completely valid method in Net Express :-

- user presses Pushbutton to re-display a Listbox in sequence by name; 
bearing in mind the list may already be sequenced by name. So the event 
from the Dialog makes a callback to the following method in the Business 
Control class.

WORKING-STORAGE SECTION.

01 SequenceFlag		pic 9.
   88 ByName		value 1.
   88 ByKey              value 2.
   88 ByOther		value 3.	*> ByOther - if there is one

*>-----------------------------------------------------------------
Method-id. "ByName".
*>-----------------------------------------------------------------

if not Byname
    set Byname to true
    invoke self "createCollection
End-if

End Method "ByName".
*>------------------------------------------------------------------

The quotes around "ByName" are optional - that's the style I first 
picked up on.

I need hardly add the red-tape feature existed ages ago before M/F OO. 
It applies to Procedural programs where you can chop out all sorts of 
useless junk. As to the method above, compare to one of the examples on 
OO in J4's Standard.

And that constant complaint from the outside world. "COBOL is sooooo.... 
verbose". To which you could reply , "Not if you use our 'cut-out red 
tape' feature".

--------------

Artur's latest on the intent to add additional stuff to OO syntax, 
"strongly typed" in other languages. (You could say he got quite 
'emotional' on that one :-)). I agree. Will be commenting in support. 
The more I look, I'm damn sure we never needed FACTORY in COBOL - 
doesn't do any harm now that we have it, but I don't think we ever 
needed it. Smalltalk got by quite happily with the equivalent syntax to :-

	'invoke MyClass "new" returning MyClassInstance

without a "FACTORY DIVISION".

What triggered my thoughts about Artur's paper and Factory, was quickly 
browsing some books in a store, saw SWING (Java) but next to it was 
SPRING (also Java - an enhanced approach to SWING). Briefly, authors 
said Java FACTORY was questionable under certain circumstances, and 
neatly they came up with the description - 'Factory is OK for 
*singleton* instances'.

Jimmy, Calgary AB
```

I didn't say there was anything in the language.  I said the idiom 
(common usage) and culture did that.  

Pick up a Cobol text, any Cobol text, and you are likely to see the 
above construct.  Something you are not likely to see in any another 
language text.

Check a company style guide and you are quite likely to find this 
if/continue/else approach for Cobol, but very unlikely for anything else.

It is indeed poor programming, but that was the discussion at hand.  C 
and Cobol both can be made quite readable or quite obfuscated depending 
upon the willingness of the programmer to work at clarity.

In article <sVL8f.16208$_31.11321@bignews5.bellsouth.net>, "Judson McClendon" <judmc@sunvaley0.com> writes:
> 
> Too bad COBOL doesn't have such crystal clear, never mistaken features such 
> as '=' vs '=='.

The various contributors to the language were too busy adding crystal-
clear, never-mistaken features such as abbreviated conditions and the
scope-closing behavior of ".".

> And the subtleties of C-style pointers makes COBOL 
> programmers pine for that type of impossible-to-misunderstand clarity.

Whereas pointers are never used incorrectly in COBOL.

C certainly has its problems.  Ritchie himself has claimed that
default fall-through in switch cases was a mistake.  Many people feel
that "=" for assignment is excessively dangerous.  The standard
library contains far too many functions which are difficult or
impossible to use safely.  And so on.  But comparisons with C do
nothing to demonstrate that COBOL - in the abstract, or as it is used
- does not have problems of its own.

   For the human reader it might make no difference if the contents of

program (please remember that the date of a file can be changed  
easily, but its content not).

declared obsolete.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol work?

_(reply depth: 13)_

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2005-10-30T10:30:31-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1130697031.378676.226740@g44g2000cwa.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<9gs5Zo$eflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>`
- **References:** `<9OV3f.23230$S4.19969@edtnps84> <c7d6f.5173$V67.1408@bignews6.bellsouth.net> <joe_zitzelberger-E34590.00352129102005@ispnews.usenetserver.com> <9gs5Zo$eflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>`

```
> But having such headers makes it easier to process such comments by
> a machine, er, a computer program.

It is no less easy to process a line with *author than one with author.

In many of my systems there are comments throughout the program which
are bounded by *<html> and *</html> tags which are extracted to create
the user documentation. These comments may be anywhere, including the
ID, and explain the business rules and how to operate the program.

By having the commentary next to the code it is much more likely to be
updated correctly and the extract to the user documentation is
automatic.

> One could even think of the
> compiler putting "Author", "Date-compiled" etc into the compiled
> program

The compilers that I use do put the date compiled into the file without
any need for a specific instruction to do this, they also put in the
compiler version.

My compiled programs include a copyright notice, a date last changed
and a version which is easily done by a COPY statement at the start of
W-S. The make file will rebuild this copied file each time any compiles
are done.  Marker values in this allow the date/time and version to be
extracted by a program so that version checks are automated.

My text editor also has a macro which adds to the source the current
date/time and latest version number from a 'version' file that contains
a summary of all the changes in the system. If the file is changed it
will be stamped with date and version and the make file will ensure
that it is compiled.

> One could even think of ....

Don't just think of it, we have been doing it for decades.

One program will process all the source files collecting the version/
date-time added there automatically by the text editor to produce a
version control list.  Another program can then be used on a client
site to ensure that the compiled programs match this list.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol work?

_(reply depth: 15)_

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2005-10-31T10:25:59-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1130783159.581922.202410@z14g2000cwz.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<9gwAGrRPflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>`
- **References:** `<9OV3f.23230$S4.19969@edtnps84> <9gs5Zo$eflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <1130697031.378676.226740@g44g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <9gwAGrRPflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de>`

```
R>> and the extract to the user documentation is automatic.

>  User documentation? I doubt that the internal program documentation
is useful for the user.

I doubt that 'internal documentation' that is only intended for a
programmer is useful at all.  My documentation is stuff that is useful
to the user, such as the business rules that are implemented, and
options for the input and explanations of the output.  By having parts
of the user documentation next to the code that implements it this is
more likely (for me at least) to be updated when the code is changed,
and it is more likely to be checked to see that the code and
documentation are in agreement.

For example the program may have:

      *--Version: 4.72  19/12/04  11:05:32       <-- added by editor
macro
       IDENTIFICATION DIVISION.
       PROGRAM-ID.     pinvxxxx.
      *<title>
      * Print Invoices
      *<html>
      * Prints the Picking Slips, Invoices and Inter Branch Transfers.
      * This uses the form AR030/97.
      *
      *<h4>Changes to December 2004</h4>
      *
      * The Invoice Reference items on the delivery tab are now
      * printed higher and this will require the printers to be
      * readjusted to get a higher lineup.
      *
      *<h4> Changes to September 2002</h4>
      *
      *<ul>
      *<li> Printing of Prices is now controlled by the
      * <a href="#pprice">Price Printing option</a> which is set
      * in <a href="custmain.htm">Customer Maintenance</a>.
      *<li> ....
      *</ul>
      *</html>
       ... ...
       PROCEDURE DIVISION.
       ... ...
       Print-Item-Line.
             ...
      *<html>
      *<h4><a name="pprice">Print Prices</a></h4>
      *
      * Prices are printed on the Picking/Packing Slips for Retail
      * Customers (Customer type 'R') and where the
      * Customer has requested that these be printed which is
      * indicated by setting the
      * <a href="custmain.htm#pprice">Price Printing option</a>
      * to All (A) or Packing (P).
      *</html>
           IF ( Document-Type = Picking-Slip
             AND ( Cust-Price-Print = "A" OR "P"
                   OR Cust-Type = "R" )
                )
                 .....

Sometimes I have had to reorder the program paragraphs to get the user
documentation extracted in a meaningful way, but that is not an issue
as my style has no dependency on the order that the paragraphs have
(except the first).
```

- **From:** "Oliver Wong" <owong@castortech.com>
- **Date:** 2005-10-31T20:12:07+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<rUu9f.67486$S4.48146@edtnps84>`
- **References:** `<9OV3f.23230$S4.19969@edtnps84> <9gs5Zo$eflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <1130697031.378676.226740@g44g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <9gwAGrRPflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <1130783344.969913.229930@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com>`

```

"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:1130783344.969913.229930@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com...
>r> It is no less easy to process a line with *author than one with
> r> author.
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> against the HR database ?

    This is actually an issue that has come up for me and my project in 
developing a program that can "understand" COBOL programs. If the author of 
the program has actually used the AUTHOR keyword, I can assume that the next 
line, in some way, identifies the author of the program. That is because the 
COBOL standard specified a certain use for the AUTHOR keyword.

    With a generic comment, if I see that the first word of a comment line 
is "author", I can't reasonably assume that the following words identify the 
author of the program in some way, or at least not with the same degree of 
certainty as in the previous situation. The problem now is that if the 
author chooses to encode authoring information at all, there are an infinite 
ways of doing so:

    * AUTHOR: Oliver

    * author: Oliver

    * Author: Oliver

    * <author name="Oliver"/>

    * @author Oliver

    * Written By: Oliver

    So what my program does is this: If it encounters the AUTHOR keyword, it 
can claim to know who the author is. Otherwise, it doesn't even bother 
trying to parse the comments and just lists the author as unknown.

    - Oliver
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol work?

_(reply depth: 18)_

- **From:** "Oliver Wong" <owong@castortech.com>
- **Date:** 2005-10-31T22:00:07+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<Htw9f.68281$S4.57655@edtnps84>`
- **References:** `<9OV3f.23230$S4.19969@edtnps84> <9gs5Zo$eflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <1130697031.378676.226740@g44g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <9gwAGrRPflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <1130783344.969913.229930@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com> <rUu9f.67486$S4.48146@edtnps84> <uPv9f.1950$vc4.1555@fe05.news.easynews.com>`

```
"William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote in message 
news:uPv9f.1950$vc4.1555@fe05.news.easynews.com...
> Just out of curiosity, does your program handle "continued" AUTHOR 
> information, e.g.
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> "hyphen-in-column-7' is NOT allowed)

    I reformatted your program as so:
<code>
       Identification Division.
       Author.  Now
                 is the time
                  for all.
                  Good men to come to the aid of their party?
       Date-Compiled. This is replaced by real date.
      *Remarks.  Start here.
</code>

And it complains about a missing Program-ID. If I then change the program as

<code>
       Identification Division.
       Program-ID. ContinuedAuthorProgram.
       Author.  Now
                 is the time
                  for all.
                  Good men to come to the aid of their party?
       Date-Compiled. This is replaced by real date.
      *Remarks.  Start here.
</code>

This is the XML representation of the parse tree:

<CompilationUnit>
  <ProgramUnit>
    <IdentificationDivision>
      <ProgramIdParagraph programName="ContinuedAuthorProgram">
      </ProgramIdParagraph>
      <IdentificationDivisionParagraph>
        <AuthorParagraph>
          <CommentLine comment="Now&#x000d;&#x000a;is the 
time&#x000d;&#x000a;for all.&#x000d;&#x000a;Good men to come to the aid of 
their party?&#x000d;&#x000a;"/>
        </AuthorParagraph>
      </IdentificationDivisionParagraph>
      <IdentificationDivisionParagraph>
        <DateCompiledParagraph>
          <CommentLine comment="This is replaced by real 
date.&#x000d;&#x000a;"/>
        </DateCompiledParagraph>
      </IdentificationDivisionParagraph>
    </IdentificationDivision>
  </ProgramUnit>
</CompilationUnit>

    Where &#x000d; and &#x000a; are XML escape codes for the newline 
terminator on Windows (i.e. "\r\n"). It looks like the indentation 
information is lost, but newlines are not.

    - Oliver
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol work?

_(reply depth: 17)_

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2005-11-01T09:25:30-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<dk88eb$2dup$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<9OV3f.23230$S4.19969@edtnps84> <9gs5Zo$eflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <1130697031.378676.226740@g44g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <9gwAGrRPflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <1130783344.969913.229930@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com> <rUu9f.67486$S4.48146@edtnps84>`

```

"Oliver Wong" <owong@castortech.com> wrote in message 
news:rUu9f.67486$S4.48146@edtnps84...
>    This is actually an issue that has come up for me and my project in 
> developing a program that can "understand" COBOL programs. If the author 
> of the program has actually used the AUTHOR keyword, I can assume that the 
> next line, in some way, identifies the author of the program. That is 
> because the COBOL standard specified a certain use for the AUTHOR keyword.

The text following the period-space separator that itself follows the AUTHOR 
keyword is a "comment-entry", which is defined as being allowed to contain 
"any combination of characters from the computer's character set."    That 
includes periods as well as spaces, as I see it.

The assumption that the *next line* after the margin-A entry "AUTHOR. " 
identifies the author is not all that likely, as most people I know who have 
used the keyword put the author's name on the *same* line as the keyword, 
and there's nothing at all to prevent the programmer from placing the 
nonblank portion of the comment entry any number of source images after the 
keyword.  Expecting the next line to contain the information is not 
warranted by the specification.

Basically, the "comment-entry" associated with the "AUTHOR" keyword consists 
of all characters from just beyond the terminator for the AUTHOR keyword 
extending to the next paragraph-name in the IDENTIFICATION DIVISION, and 
absent such a paragraph, the header for the next DIVISION in the program. 
There is nothing to say that that "comment-entry" must contain non-blank 
characters.

I strongly suspect the vagueness of the definition of "comment-entry" is one 
of the reasons it, and the paragraphs that used it, were marked as obsolete 
elements of COBOL in the '85 standard.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

news:joe_zitzelberger-CA4405.04205303112005@ispnews.usenetserver.com...
> In article <rUu9f.67486$S4.48146@edtnps84>,
> "Oliver Wong" <owong@castortech.com> wrote:
…[43 more quoted lines elided]…
> subject to the authors whim.

    While it's true that you cannot know for sure that the AUTHOR paragraph 
actually contains the AUTHOR name, I think you can reasonably assume it. The 
purpose of that paragraph is documented in the COBOL standards, and if the 
author chooses to use the paragraph for any other purpose, they are 
essentially intentionally trying to mislead the reader or the compiler.

    There are tools out there that allow you to "automatically generate 
documentation" by parsing the comments. For this to work, the comments have 
to be in a specific format. Obviously, the author could lie or abuse the 
format, causing the tool to fail. But I wouldn't blame this failure on the 
tool.

    In COBOL, the division paragraph is the closest thing to a standard for 
a special format of comments to allow "automatic documentation generation". 
Comments anywhere else in the COBOL program could mean anything, but the 
"comment" that consists of the AUTHOR paragraph SHOULD give information 
about the author of the program. Again, the author could lie, causing the 
tool to fail, but I wouldn't blame that on the tool.

    - Oliver
```

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2005-11-03T16:05:00-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<dke8jc$304h$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<9OV3f.23230$S4.19969@edtnps84> <9gs5Zo$eflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <1130697031.378676.226740@g44g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <9gwAGrRPflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <1130783344.969913.229930@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com> <rUu9f.67486$S4.48146@edtnps84> <joe_zitzelberger-CA4405.04205303112005@ispnews.usenetserver.com> <NUpaf.74531$S4.9546@edtnps84> <swvaf.64900$MS3.27952@fe09.news.easynews.com>`

```

"William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote in message 
news:swvaf.64900$MS3.27952@fe09.news.easynews.com...

> P.S.  My experience has been mostly IBM mainframes and PC's (mostly Micro 
> Focus, but some Fujitsu and RM).  I would be curious if Chuck or anyone 
> else has seen it as MORE accurate in other mainframe or other COBOL 
> environments.

My experience leads me to believe it is inappropriate to make *any* 
assumption -- historical or otherwise -- about the contents of the AUTHOR 
paragraph, or any other of the comment-entries in the IDENTIFICATION 
DIVISION, for that matter, beyond the fact that there may be any number of 
non-blank characters between the terminating period-space and the next 
syntactic element (valid or otherwise) that can be found in Margin A.

For example, *must* the COBOL source code generated by a 4GL such as the 
Unisys product EAE (or its predecessor LINC) indicate that the "author" of 
the program was itself a program, and of what generation, or *must* it 
reflect the author of the specifications that led to its generation?   What 
about filter products (translation from COBOL68 to COBOL85, for example)? 
How about translators from assembler or RPG?

I've been exposed at various points to the source generated by examples of 
all of these.  Honestly, I don't remember what the conventions that any of 
them used were, except to note that those conventions were whatever the 
particular program used to produce the source decided they needed to be.  I 
would strongly recommend against assumptions as to the meaningfulness of the 
contents of the AUTHOR paragraph of any COBOL program, be those assumptions 
programmatic or observational.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol work?

_(reply depth: 18)_

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2005-11-03T09:15:24-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<dkdgjd$2hmo$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<9OV3f.23230$S4.19969@edtnps84> <9gs5Zo$eflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <1130697031.378676.226740@g44g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <9gwAGrRPflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <1130783344.969913.229930@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com> <rUu9f.67486$S4.48146@edtnps84> <joe_zitzelberger-CA4405.04205303112005@ispnews.usenetserver.com>`

```

"Joe Zitzelberger" <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote in message 
news:joe_zitzelberger-CA4405.04205303112005@ispnews.usenetserver.com...

> I think the only thing you can reasonably assume from the Identification
> division is the program name.  Everything else is truly a comment
> subject to the authors whim.

True for '85 COBOL, but not according to the current standard.

AUTHOR was marked obsolete in the '85 standard and isn't in '02 at all. 
Likewise the "comment-entry"  -- any combination of characters from the 
computer's character set -- that the AUTHOR paragraph contained is an 
obsolete element in '85 and gone from '02.

In '85 COBOL, there's nothing whatever to prevent the "AUTHOR" paragraph 
from containing a graphic representation of a portrait of the author made up 
of printable characters, so long as none of those characters occupy Margin 
A.  If there's something in Margin A, it has to be one of the defined 
IDENTIFICATION DIVISION paragraphs.

In *current* standard COBOL, the PROGRAM-ID, FUNCTION-ID, CLASS-ID, FACTORY, 
OBJECT, METHOD-ID, and INTERFACE-ID paragraphs each have specific effects on 
the generated code, as do the ARITHMETIC and ENTRY-CONVENTION clauses of the 
OPTIONS paragraph.  Anything beyond these is an implementor-defined 
extension to the IDENTIFICATION DIVISION.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

```
> Eh? On what compiler did DATE-COMPILED have to be updated manually?

The Date-Compiled paragraph in the _source_ program has to be updated
manually if it is to have anything meaningful in it.  The compiler does
not update the source file and does not need anything at all in the
source to put the date/time in the compiler listing header and object
code.

Perhaps if you had a machine without a system clock then the
'date-compiled' paragraph may have some use if it was manually updated
each time.
```

Richard,
  I am a little confused.  I thought you used Micro Focus compilers (among - 
possibly - others).  With Micro Focus, the

 DATE-COMPILED

paragraph definitely gets filled *IN THE LISTING* (and this has nothing to do 
with the header or footers on listings).

I am 99% certain that all compilers that conform to the '89 Intrinsic Function 
Amendment (and probably most that don't) also put it in the "object code" - even 
when the Function Date-Compiled intrinsic function isn't used.  However, I don't 
think there is any ANSI/ISO requirement for this (as there is for filling in the 
Identification Division paragraph - when code).

_(reply depth: 15)_

- **From:** Joe Zitzelberger <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com>
- **Date:** 2005-11-03T03:57:25-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<joe_zitzelberger-E1E13E.03572503112005@ispnews.usenetserver.com>`
- **References:** `<9OV3f.23230$S4.19969@edtnps84> <c7d6f.5173$V67.1408@bignews6.bellsouth.net> <joe_zitzelberger-E34590.00352129102005@ispnews.usenetserver.com> <9gs5Zo$eflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <joe_zitzelberger-404417.22294430102005@ispnews.usenetserver.com> <akjbm19f23cva5tej797iad2ed91daloia@4ax.com>`

```
In article <akjbm19f23cva5tej797iad2ed91daloia@4ax.com>,
 Ian Dalziel <iandalziel@lineone.net> wrote:

> On Sun, 30 Oct 2005 22:29:51 -0500, Joe Zitzelberger
> <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote:
…[37 more quoted lines elided]…
> Eh? On what compiler did DATE-COMPILED have to be updated manually?

It never needed to be updated manually, it never needed to be updated at 
all.  That is the problem -- it should not be maintainable by just any 
old Tom, Dick or Harry because they might lie about the last 
DATE-COMPILED.

One could, for example, enter this text into a source program:

   AUTHOR.        Mycroft Holmes
   INSTALLATION.  Luna City
   DATE-WRITTEN.  11-11-2012
   DATE-COMPILED. 11-25-2012

There is nothing that assures the reader of said source that any of the 
above is accurate.  

That is why a well-meaning compiler should stamp the userid of the 
person compiling the program into the executable.  As well as the date 
it was done.  And perhaps the machine address.  But it should never rely 
on the source comments provided in the DATE-COMPILED paragraph, because 
those could be pure fiction.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Cobol work?

_(reply depth: 14)_

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2005-10-31T08:28:40-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<dk5gno$oo4$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<9OV3f.23230$S4.19969@edtnps84> <c7d6f.5173$V67.1408@bignews6.bellsouth.net> <joe_zitzelberger-E34590.00352129102005@ispnews.usenetserver.com> <9gs5Zo$eflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <joe_zitzelberger-404417.22294430102005@ispnews.usenetserver.com>`

```

"Joe Zitzelberger" <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote in message 
news:joe_zitzelberger-404417.22294430102005@ispnews.usenetserver.com...

> IBM's Cobol, to cite one example, always embeds the actual system date
> and time an executable is generated.  It is easily machine readable.
> The text paragraph is less than useless, since it might provide
> erroneous information.

There are actually *three* time/date values to worry about in compilation: 
the time at which DATE-COMPILED was processed; the time at which the 
date/time value is written into the object code file; and the time/date at 
which the object code file is created (more of an issue for multipass 
compilers than for single-pass compilers).

Unisys MCP COBOL compilers go to some trouble to make sure the time recorded 
in the listing and the time recorded in the code file are exactly the same. 
The codefile creation timestamp itself may differ slightly.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

_(reply depth: 8)_

- **From:** Joe Zitzelberger <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com>
- **Date:** 2005-10-29T00:28:27-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<joe_zitzelberger-295F9D.00282729102005@ispnews.usenetserver.com>`
- **References:** `<9OV3f.23230$S4.19969@edtnps84> <20051015132907.058$g2@news.newsreader.com> <pan.2005.10.16.03.08.37.117719@starband.net> <11l5em9nkca7318@news.supernews.com> <dj0guj0k2f@news4.newsguy.com> <hrn7l1daddpu1m368c3mpievfad47tt24v@4ax.com> <OZadnYRVUOPeKcveRVn-ig@adelphia.com> <4iz5f.37655$S4.20966@edtnps84>`

```
In article <4iz5f.37655$S4.20966@edtnps84>,
 "Oliver Wong" <owong@castortech.com> wrote:

> "John Culleton" <john@wexfordpress.com> wrote in message 
> news:OZadnYRVUOPeKcveRVn-ig@adelphia.com...
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> in their (incontestable) opinion.

There is a common Cobol convention that attempts to remove as much  
meaningfulness from names as possible.

It is very common to find programs where EVERY declared variable is 
prefixed by "WS-".  The effect of such tautologies is only to reduce the 
namespace, and thus the possible meaningfulness, by three characters.

But my personal pet peeve is the idea that some people have that a 
number provides some form of uniqueness.  A common example, using 
paragraph names:

   1000-READ-FILE.
   2000-READ-FILE.

Often the defenders of such crap will staunchly oppose the clear and 
meaningful approach of:

   READ-MASTER-FILE.
   READ-TRANSACTION-FILE.

There was once a (somewhat) useful purpose behind both of these.  The 
data name prefix did help locate variables when you got your compile 
listing back after your deck was submitted.  And the numeric prefix on 
the labels helped maintain order when the only control structure 
available was the GO TO.  But that was a long time ago.

Cobol culture and standard practices do anything but encourage 
meaningful names, the encourage some level of meaninglessness in support 
of conventions that have had no practical purpose for 30 years or so.
```

On Sat, 29 Oct 2005 00:28:27 -0400, Joe Zitzelberger
<joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote:

>   1000-READ-FILE.
>   2000-READ-FILE.
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
>of conventions that have had no practical purpose for 30 years or so.

One other advantage of having a numeric prefix was the ability to type
in the TSO/SPF command

f p'#' 8 prev

This finds the previous occurrence of a number in column 8 - which
should be the paragraph name.

A klutz, but a useful one in programs with lots of comments.    A
smarter CoBOL aware editor might have a "find previous paragraph name"
command, but I'm unaware of such.
```

_(reply depth: 9)_

- **From:** "Judson McClendon" <judmc@sunvaley0.com>
- **Date:** 2005-10-29T12:01:35-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<TTN8f.16242$_31.8441@bignews5.bellsouth.net>`
- **References:** `<9OV3f.23230$S4.19969@edtnps84> <20051015132907.058$g2@news.newsreader.com> <pan.2005.10.16.03.08.37.117719@starband.net> <11l5em9nkca7318@news.supernews.com> <dj0guj0k2f@news4.newsguy.com> <hrn7l1daddpu1m368c3mpievfad47tt24v@4ax.com> <OZadnYRVUOPeKcveRVn-ig@adelphia.com> <4iz5f.37655$S4.20966@edtnps84> <joe_zitzelberger-295F9D.00282729102005@ispnews.usenetserver.com>`

```
"Joe Zitzelberger" <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote:
>
> It is very common to find programs where EVERY declared variable is
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
> of conventions that have had no practical purpose for 30 years or so.

I believe you have a limited view, my friend. :-) The reasons above are not 
the only ones for those conventions. I do agree that preceding *every* 
WORKING STORAGE data item with "WS-" is nutty.

The convention today is to break applications down into very small programs, 
but sometimes there are practical reasons of system architecture that make 
fewer, larger programs desirable. In an online program that handles 30 or 50 
different screens, it makes perfect sense to prefix every paragraph that 
handles screen 12, for example, with 12nnnn-, and place '12' in the prefix 
of every WORKING STORAGE data item for that screen. In such a program there 
may be quite a number of READ-MASTER-FILE paragraphs, all very similar, 
though likely not identical, and the '12nnnn-' type of paragraph prefix 
identifies them perfectly. And if you have 5000 lines of DATA DIVISION, 
prefix of 'WS-' meaning a 77 level can be very useful. Sure, you can find 
any data item quickly with your source editor, but why do even that, if the 
name can tell what you need to know? This is so simple and powerful, I am 
amazed that it isn't very obvious to you.

I sometimes build systems with hundreds of thousands, even millions, of 
lines of code and many files. In such a system, I create name and 
abbreviation dictionaries so that every word used in data/paragraph names is 
spelled or abbreviated in *exactly* the same way in every use. Data names 
for the same field never vary, except for a prefix that designates the 
record (or "WS-" for 77 levels) containing the field, or when the sense of 
the field changes (e.g. Current Balance -> Previous Balance). My systems are 
crafted as carefully as I can build them. Nothing is left to chance that I 
can prevent, and I go to great lengths to make them understandable and 
maintainable. In my whole career (since '68) have never once delivered a 
system that a client was less than very happy with, and typically, after 
three or four weeks eliminating the few final bugs, I don't hear from the 
clients for months or years, until they want something new. Two weeks ago I 
fixed a bug in one of my systems that another programmer put there, and 
that's the first bug in a deployed system I've fixed for over a year, and 
that one was by the same programmer. If you have programming methods that 
produce better results than this archaic, out-of-date methodology that I 
use, then please tell me about them. :-)

Just because the reason for something isn't obvious to you, doesn't mean 
there isn't a reason, even a good one. The real world is messy and chaotic. 
Programming real-world applications so that they are clear and 
understandable takes hard work, discipline, and careful adherence to 
practical methodology, things we see too little of these days. :-)
```

…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
> writing, which is the real work. :-)

I think that often deploying can be _more_ complicated than some software
design and writing.  A typical enterprise installation requires multiple
hardware systems, not one single point of failure (that is redundant
storage, cpus, databases, power, offices and device drivers) and that
doesn't even begin to discuss the need to determine the resources and people
needed to manage and run the operations after the deployment.  Depends
highly on the breadth of the application, of course, but when it is your end
to end supply chain....and that doesn't even touch on the maintenance and
management of the security to all the layers of the system...and that
doesn't even begin to touch on the management of the OS and software and
updates (even Linux has updates).

It ain't a piece of cake...I think unfortunately there is a tendency for
execs (this is not implying I think you are an exec, or that I even have you
are in mind - after all I don't know you) to think that you pay, you deploy,
you watch the cost savings without understanding what that means long term.
There are savings to be had, and more often than not, efficiencies to be
gained....but there is still a price for that and it's often in length of
contract, organizational restructure, business process realignment etc.

As that's not my thing at the moment though, I'm going to agree with you.

>>> Data names for the same field never vary, except for a prefix that 
>>> designates the record (or "WS-" for 77 levels) containing the field, or 
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
> happy to refer you to the responsible parties, if you want to check. :-)
No, but if I'm ever lucky enough to have my own business with my own
requirements I might take you up on that.

>>  I also believe that the hardest bugs to find are the ones that create 
>> systematically incorrect but consistent results.  Bug Free code does 
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
> that? But they all love the system. :-)

69444.45 LOC / month

2314.815 LOC/ day
Assume 1440 minutes a day because sleeping is optional:
2314.82  / 1440 =1.61

6-7

of every day for 18 months.

house/office, spend an hour at clc....the rate to sustaing per working
minute would no doubt go up.....

"news-server.tampabay.rr.com" <defaultuser@hotmail.com> wrote:

>> "Defaultuser" <Defaultuser@hotmail.com> wrote:

I said initially that I had one associate to help, experienced and highly

and business programming. The third guy just helped with writing reports the

>> When I'm really cranking can I sometimes write up to 4,000 LOC/hr by
>> hand, though I can't maintain that rate all the time, of course.
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> with you when you're cranking.

When I am programming onsite, sometimes other programmers will gather around
and watch me write/edit code. One of my good friends used to be a manager of
data entry, and he was faster than any of his data entry people. He says I
am the fastest person on the keyboard he ever saw. But this is only when
programming. When I am just typing text, like now, I fairly poke along. But
there's more than that: I really work at being efficient. For example, if I
am writing a block of code that moves a series of fields from a database to
Working Storage, I will copy and past the field names from the database
description to where the MOVEs will be, then use my editor's key-macro to
insert the upper case part below for each line:

    MOVE DBPFX-<pasted field name> TO WSPFX-<pasted field name>

If there are several fields, this is far faster than typing them, and far
more accurate.

…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> comment, I promise.

if no one objects.

If any program handles 30 to 50 screens it has completely missed the 
concept of modular software.  

In my mere 27 years of software construction I have never found a case 
where it was desirable to have fewer, larger, programs.  (I will not say 
that I haven't written such, but it was never desirable)  Such 
monolithic beasts violate every principle of of modern software 
engineering.


> I sometimes build systems with hundreds of thousands, even millions, of 
> lines of code and many files. In such a system, I create name and 
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> use, then please tell me about them. :-)

I do indeed have programming methods that produce more stable, easier to 
maintain code: Modular programming and Object oriented thought 
processes.  Both will help to insulate you from haphazard maintenance 
programmers -- even in non-OO languages.

Not only is a nice, clean, OO implementation blindingly obvious for a 
maintenance programmer to understand, but the limited scope of 
everything tends to protects it from even those that don't get it.


> Just because the reason for something isn't obvious to you, doesn't mean 
> there isn't a reason, even a good one. The real world is messy and chaotic. 
> Programming real-world applications so that they are clear and 
> understandable takes hard work, discipline, and careful adherence to 
> practical methodology, things we see too little of these days. :-)

I specifically complained about the ubiquitous use of "WS-" for 
everything not in a copybook -- a common shop and textbook standard BTW.

I know that you make meaningful use of prefixes, so do I.  I was 
complaining about silly conventions that avoid meaningful use as you 
describe it above and require a single, universal, prefix to be used 
everywhere.

If there is some other reason for universal WS- prefixing other than 
grouping your fields in the compile listing XREF it certainly is not 
obvious to me.  I am curious to know what it is.

- **References:** `<9OV3f.23230$S4.19969@edtnps84> <joe_zitzelberger-295F9D.00282729102005@ispnews.usenetserver.com> <TTN8f.16242$_31.8441@bignews5.bellsouth.net> <joe_zitzelberger-249AC9.03402603112005@ispnews.usenetserver.com> <dkcofk$gin$1@reader2.panix.com>`

```
In article <dkcofk$gin$1@reader2.panix.com>, docdwarf@panix.com () 
wrote:

> In article <joe_zitzelberger-249AC9.03402603112005@ispnews.usenetserver.com>,
> Joe Zitzelberger  <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote:
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
> DD

I would present a program I was looking at earlier today as evidence.  
It was one of these old-school, 300,000 line monoliths with thousands of 
WORKING-STORAGE fields in defined in the source module itself.

Absolutely every field in WORKING-STORAGE was prefixed "WS-".

To make my case I will further qualify that there were many field 
qualified "WS-DB2-" to indicate DB2 related working storage fields.  
There were "WS-CNT-" to indicate counter fields.  And even "WS-WK-" to 
indicate temporary working variables.  And so on... Absolutely every 
field was prefixed with "WS-".

My contention is that a trained eye can more easily identify:

   DB2-blah-blah
   WK-blah-blah 
   CNT-blah-blah

than it can identify:

   WS-DB2-blah-blah
   WS-WK-blah-blah 
   WS-CNT-blah-blah

The second case violates the brains ability to distinguish patterns 
because it enforces 'sameness' upon the beginning.
```

```
"Joe Zitzelberger" <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote:
> "Judson McClendon" <judmc@sunvaley0.com> wrote:
>>
…[27 more quoted lines elided]…
> engineering.

In my 37 years of software construction, I have. :-)  Did you miss the part 
about "reasons of system architecture?" In the case of the vehicle licensing 
system I described earlier, we used a COBOL74 instead of a COBOL85 compiler 
because first, the COBOL85 compiler was not nearly as stable and bug-free as 
the COBOL74 compiler, and second, the COBOL74 compiler compiled several 
times as fast as the COBOL85 compiler. That specific COBOL74 compiler did 
not support easily splitting the code up into smaller modules as you 
suggest. As it was, we were under the gun timewise from the start. Choosing 
a compiler or techniques that would slow us down or intruduce reliability 
uncertainties would have been suicide, no matter what the theoretical design 
advantages might have been. Welcome to the real world.

>> I sometimes build systems with hundreds of thousands, even millions, of
>> lines of code and many files. In such a system, I create name and
…[29 more quoted lines elided]…
> everything tends to protects it from even those that don't get it.

Does it protect it from bugs in the OO modules you use that you can't access 
the source for because it's hidden by OO? :-)

The fact is, structured design and programming proved themselves beyond any 
reasonable doubt to anyone who was paying attention within 10 years. After 
more than 20 years, OO hasn't even come close to showing universal 
advantage. If it had not been pushed by academia, who are and always have 
been clueless about practicality, having to meet no objective standards 
themselves, and a few big noises like Microsoft, OO would have never been 
more than a flash in the pan. If OO had been anywhere near as good as its 
proponents claim, only rarely would anybody be using anything else by now. 
That was certainly the case less than 20 years after structured techniques 
were introduced. That's not to say that OO is 'bad', but just like GUI and 
client/server, none of them are 'best for all applications'. :-)

But the most important question, to me is: "Does it give better results than 
my own current techniques?" I really, really, doubt it. ;-)
```

Nothing about Object-Oriented programming involves "hiding" source

OO is an approach to structuring programs.

> If it had not been pushed by academia,

The biggest proponents of OO - language inventors like Alan Kay,
Bjarne Stroustrop, and James Gosling on the one hand, and proponents
of various coding practices like Scott Meyers, Grady Booch, and the
Design Patterns "Gang of Four" (Erich Gamma et al) - are not
academics.  Some of them are researchers, but they work in industry
labs.

That's a gross slur, even for you.

> having to meet no objective standards themselves,

Clearly you know nothing about academia.

> OO would have never been more than a flash in the pan.

Astounding ignorance.  Name a general-purpose programming language

popular demand.  If you think that's the effect of pressure from

You're living in a fantasy.  Software development is not some magic

couple of days to see ample evidence of that.

"Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:
>> That specific COBOL74 compiler did
>> not support easily splitting the code up into smaller modules
>
> In what way did it not ?

It's been nearly ten years, but IIRC, it was that the Unisys A Series 
COBOL74 compiler, circa 1996, did not support subprograms, or had some 
problems with using them. Chuck Stephens is much more intimately familiar 
with internals of that compiler than I, perhaps he will chime in. Anyway, 
because of this and other factors (perhaps departmental standards), the 
client instructed us to write the programs that way. As it was, we had 7 
online programs. Writing several dozen separate online programs would not 
have been acceptable, either.

Understand, I am not advocating a monolithic program approach. But hard, 
inflexible rules like "always do this" or "never do that" or "this is always 
better than that" often come into conflict with real world practicalities. 
The real world can be extremely chaotic and messy. One of the most 
aggravating things I have had to deal with is when requirements are 
determined by human laws, which can be extremely illogical, even mutually 
contradictory. You might be able to persuade an irrational manager, but you 
can't persuade a law. :-)

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2005-11-04T15:19:45-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<dkgqai$1gdk$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<9OV3f.23230$S4.19969@edtnps84> <20051015132907.058$g2@news.newsreader.com> <pan.2005.10.16.03.08.37.117719@starband.net> <11l5em9nkca7318@news.supernews.com> <dj0guj0k2f@news4.newsguy.com> <hrn7l1daddpu1m368c3mpievfad47tt24v@4ax.com> <OZadnYRVUOPeKcveRVn-ig@adelphia.com> <4iz5f.37655$S4.20966@edtnps84> <joe_zitzelberger-295F9D.00282729102005@ispnews.usenetserver.com> <TTN8f.16242$_31.8441@bignews5.bellsouth.net> <joe_zitzelberger-249AC9.03402603112005@ispnews.usenetserver.com> <tvqaf.38322$ty1.7855@bignews1.bellsouth.net> <1131133870.689357.315820@g49g2000cwa.googlegroups.com> <p3Raf.41895$ty1.30790@bignews1.bellsouth.net>`

```
Note that Unisys MCP COBOL74 *does* support ANSI-74 IPC CALL statements as 
well as some implementor-defined additional features.  The limitation was 
that each called COBOL74 routine had to be a separately-compiled program.

Using this, and presuming you use CALL <literal> and not CALL <dataname>, 
and presuming you didn't do CANCEL much, once you got the first CALL on a 
program done, subsequent CALLs are really efficient.

Note that COBOL85 has extensions that allow the declaration of and calls to 
multiple-entry-point libraries as well as multithreaded and recursive calls 
in that language.

CALL <dataname> is not a good way to do things in the Unisys MCP environment 
(in COBOL85 as well as COBOL74).   It works, it meets the ANSI requirements, 
but from an overhead standpoint there's virtually no case in which having 
hundreds or even thousands of different CALL <literal> statements, and the 
associated execution-time logic to choose from among them, would be less 
efficient ...   I have heard there are implementations in which CALL 
<dataname> is preferable to CALL <literal>, but the Unisys MCP environment 
is not one of them.

    -Chuck Stevens


"Judson McClendon" <judmc@sunvaley0.com> wrote in message

_(reply depth: 12)_

- **From:** "Richard" <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2005-11-04T12:20:13-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1131135613.697703.281010@o13g2000cwo.googlegroups.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<tvqaf.38322$ty1.7855@bignews1.bellsouth.net>`
- **References:** `<9OV3f.23230$S4.19969@edtnps84> <20051015132907.058$g2@news.newsreader.com> <pan.2005.10.16.03.08.37.117719@starband.net> <11l5em9nkca7318@news.supernews.com> <dj0guj0k2f@news4.newsguy.com> <hrn7l1daddpu1m368c3mpievfad47tt24v@4ax.com> <OZadnYRVUOPeKcveRVn-ig@adelphia.com> <4iz5f.37655$S4.20966@edtnps84> <joe_zitzelberger-295F9D.00282729102005@ispnews.usenetserver.com> <TTN8f.16242$_31.8441@bignews5.bellsouth.net> <joe_zitzelberger-249AC9.03402603112005@ispnews.usenetserver.com> <tvqaf.38322$ty1.7855@bignews1.bellsouth.net>`

```
> If OO had been anywhere near as good as its
> proponents claim, only rarely would anybody be using anything else by now.

That is just silly. For example I doubt that you would use it, ever,
and thus you could claim that was 'proof' that OO was not as good as
its proponents claim, otherwise you would be using it.

OO was developed in the Simula language to deal with specific problems
that would be much more complicated to deal with in other ways.  C++
adopted classes, overloading and inheretence to overcome problems in C,
many of which did not occur in Cobol, nor in the applications for which
Cobol has traditionally been used.

There is almost no point at all in using OO for a batch program that is
sequentially processing one record at a time.  That problem is one of
serial reuse.

Where OO is really useful, even in Cobol, is where processing of
multiple objects is required which are of the same 'class'.  For
example I use templating a lot for output and this is all done through
a templating module.  If the module is a subroutine then it will handle
one output at a time. It would need extensive reworking to be able to
handle multiple concurrent outputs as all the data areas would require
duplicating. Or I would need to have two or three versions of the code
compiled with different names to have a different callable module for
each output.  So if I wanted to to have one program that output a
packing slip,  despatch labels, and an EMail advice (as in fact I do
have), I would either have to create three modules, enhance the module
to use three sets of data areas, or serially reuse one module reloading
the templates for each document (and thus incurring an overhead).

With OO it is only necessary to create a new object from the one class
to do this.  A new set of data areas are created automatically while
the code only needs to deal with what it thinks is just one set.

OO need not be used for everything, but it does have useful facility.
When code is in a class it sometimes becomes surprising how easy it is
to solve a new problem compared with how it would have had to be done
using other techniques.

> That was certainly the case less than 20 years after structured techniques
> were introduced.

That certainly isn't true. Depending of what people call 'structured
techniques' I still find much recent code that I would not call
'structured'.
```

Nothing is hidden by OO at the source level.  And using OO-style 
construction techniques with Cobol really hide nothing, only what the 
programmer has discipline to hide.

Such discipline, well applied, will prevent maintenance programmers from 
making a total mess though...by making it harder to muck up things they 
shouldn't me messing with.

> The fact is, structured design and programming proved themselves beyond any

Yes.

I can unequivocally say that my methods yield more readable source code.  
After all, there tends to be about 1/5th as many source lines when I get 
through revamping a program.  That means that a maintenance programmer 
needs only spend 1/5th the time trying to wrap his brain around the 
source.

Noise in ones source is nothing more than noise.  It is less than 
useless, it is a great waste of time.

- **References:** `<9OV3f.23230$S4.19969@edtnps84> <20051015132907.058$g2@news.newsreader.com> <pan.2005.10.16.03.08.37.117719@starband.net> <11l5em9nkca7318@news.supernews.com> <dj0guj0k2f@news4.newsguy.com> <hrn7l1daddpu1m368c3mpievfad47tt24v@4ax.com> <OZadnYRVUOPeKcveRVn-ig@adelphia.com> <4iz5f.37655$S4.20966@edtnps84> <joe_zitzelberger-295F9D.00282729102005@ispnews.usenetserver.com>`

```
"Joe Zitzelberger" <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote in message 
news:joe_zitzelberger-295F9D.00282729102005@ispnews.usenetserver.com...
> In article <4iz5f.37655$S4.20966@edtnps84>,
> "Oliver Wong" <owong@castortech.com> wrote:
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
>> in their (incontestable) opinion.

Actually - knowing some professors this is becoming less common.  Today's 
spoiled children do not like being told they are wrong.  This leads to two 
types of common questions: those with undisputable answers (what is 1 + 1 
using standard integer math as taught by me) and those that get marked as 
working or not working.  In fact these days you get given a set of "test 
cases" and it is the passing of those test cases that dictates how well you 
did...not any style.

> There is a common Cobol convention that attempts to remove as much
> meaningfulness from names as possible.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> namespace, and thus the possible meaningfulness, by three characters.

There can sometimes be benefit to using "WS-" versus "LS-" or "DB2-" or 
"IMS-"  "GL-" "EX-" to state the scope or source.  I find the benefit 
limiting because for some reason COBOL programmers are obsessive about data 
moves and it all ends up being modified everywhere anyway.
I'm not fussed about any naming convention as long as it's accurate. I don't 
care if someone uses
lpsz as a long pointer to a null terminated string or not....as long as 
that's what it is.  I don't ask for much, just consistency.

COBOL mentality...
SELECT INTO :DB-VALUE FROM DB2
MOVE DB-VALUE TO WS-VALUE

Not sure why it now needs to be in yet another variable, but whatever.  See 
it all the time.

> But my personal pet peeve is the idea that some people have that a
> number provides some form of uniqueness.  A common example, using
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>   2000-READ-FILE.

It also makes for amusement when making code changes.....
1000-READ-FILE
1500-READ-RECORD
1550-PROCESS-RECORD
1555-ADD-DATA-TO-RECORD
1555-POINT-5-ADD-ONE-MORE-THING

hmmmm...then (for the ISPF users)
C all 1000- 10000-
C all 1500- 15000-
C all 1550- 15500-
C all 1555- 15550-

C all 1555-POINT-5- 15555-

All fixed.

> Often the defenders of such crap will staunchly oppose the clear and
> meaningful approach of:
>
>   READ-MASTER-FILE.
>   READ-TRANSACTION-FILE.

That's good as long as that paragraph is reading the file and doing nothing 
more...otherwise it's misleading.  Paragraphs that state what they are doing 
shouldn't do more than that.

> There was once a (somewhat) useful purpose behind both of these.  The
> data name prefix did help locate variables when you got your compile
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> of conventions that have had no practical purpose for 30 years or so.
I was sarcastic with my statement about COBOL programmers.  I sense that you 
are not.  What documented standard practices are you referring to that do 
anything but encourage meaningful names? I think that it's more often to do 
with the "cut and paste" nature that exists in most development shops.  I've 
never written a prolog or identification division or file division in my 
life.  I copy them.  More than likely from someone who is "top-dog" in the 
office.

My personal pet peeve is the use of upper case to write all code.  I think 
that about 70% of reading is performed by "scanning" the shape of the 
letters.  Uppercase code is harder to read than lower case code which is why 
XML generally is mixed/lower case.

IF YOU DONT BELIEVE WHAT I AM SAYING THEN YOU SHOULD CONSIDER HOW MUCH 
HARDER IT IS TO READ THIS SHORT SENTENCE PART than it is to read this small 
sentence part.  The brain is a magical thing....shame we abuse it :-)


D
```

    When reading COBOL code, I actually prefer that the keywords be written 
in all upper case, and everything else (e.g. variable names) be written in 
lower case. I think I'm recognizing the shapes of the words, and I associate 
the COBOL concept of an unconditional branch with the 
pixel-array-configuration that look like "GO TO" and not with the 
pixel-array-configuration that looks like "go to".

    - Oliver

```
In article <j9Z8f.221646$p_1.213624@tornado.tampabay.rr.com>,
 "Defaultuser" <Defaultuser@hotmail.com> wrote:
> 
> > There was once a (somewhat) useful purpose behind both of these.  The
…[15 more quoted lines elided]…
> office.

Given a limited number of characters to describe a paragraph or a 
variable, anything that consumes those characters without adding 
uniqueness is reducing the possible meaningfulness.

Also, as you have mentioned, we do not simply read things, we look for 
patterns, first and last characters matter more in some cases.  Our 
brains can pick up on things faster than we can parse the individual 
letters, but common prefixing can work to defeat that ability.

And I'm not opposed to prefixing, I'm just opposed to prefixing 
everything with the same thing.  Putting "WS" before everything makes it 
hard for the brains automatic recognition to work.  If things are 
differently prefixed, that isn't such a problem.

Paragraph numbers are worse as the tend to be longer.

   A1000-Something
   A1010-Something
   A1101-Something

Require careful reading to recognize the differences.


> My personal pet peeve is the use of upper case to write all code.  I think 
> that about 70% of reading is performed by "scanning" the shape of the 
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> sentence part.  The brain is a magical thing....shame we abuse it :-)

I couldn't agree more.  Case sensitive languages are a wonderful thing 
-- they don't allow such mess outside of comments.
```

```
"Defaultuser" <Defaultuser@hotmail.com> wrote:
>
> My personal pet peeve is the use of upper case to write all code.  I think 
> that about 70% of reading is performed by "scanning" the shape of the 
> letters.  Uppercase code is harder to read than lower case code which is 
> why XML generally is mixed/lower case.

I have read, though I don't know if it's true, that in the early days of 
teletype they were going to use lower case text for legibility until someone 
asked "What about God?," so they decided to go upper case.

In 1968 when I started programming, everyone used upper case all the time 
because they had no choice; most keypunch machines didn't support lower 
case. From force of habit, I suppose, I still program COBOL in all upper 
case, except for text in quotes and comments. For other languages, like 
BASIC, C, Pascal and assembly, I follow pretty standard convention.
```

```

In article <ZD2af.29608$wG.27339@bignews4.bellsouth.net>, "Judson McClendon" <judmc@sunvaley0.com> writes:
> 
> I have read, though I don't know if it's true, that in the early days of 
> teletype they were going to use lower case text for legibility until someone 
> asked "What about God?," so they decided to go upper case.

Older versions of the Jargon File provide a variant of this story:
supposedly it was at Teletype Corporation where a study had shown
that, for a monocase device, lower case was easier to read; but the
chairman rejected that decision because "It would be impossible to
spell the name of the Deity correctly".  (It labelled this story
folklore.)

However, in the current version, ESR claims that this story has been
disproved, and that "the upper-case interpretation of teleprinter
codes was well established by 1870".[1]

Unfortunately, he doesn't provide any information on why upper case
might have been selected originally.  That's probably lost in the
mists of time...


[1] http://www.catb.org/~esr/jargon/html/G/Great-Runes.html
```

What is 'harder' or 'easier' is entirely what one is used to.

More specificly, what is 'hard' or 'easy' is what one has trained the
brain to do for you.

If Cobol programmers have only, or mainly, used all upper case then
their brains will be attuned to reading Cobol that way and may have
difficulty relating to code in lower case.

any way indicate that Richard is now "King Richard".  Besides, why 
change identities if you've built up a 6-year participation record?

>>MW did not call you one,
> 
> Ermm, what planet do you live on ?  He specifically called me a "newbie".

He specifically *did not* call *you* a newbie - he said you had 
committed every newbie sin in one post.  He was critiquing the content 
of your post, not you as a person.

(And, Richard lives in probably one of the most beautiful places on a 
planet called Earth.)

>>but he is quite correct: you did commit every newbie sin.
> 
> LOL !!  His post was simply too rediculous to carve up or snip.

And of what benefit was your comment?  Okay, you think he's wrong, he's 
missed the boat.  That's fine - but tell him *what* you think is wrong 
with the post.  Quoting a long post, with a single line at the bottom 
saying something to the effect of "you're full of crap" makes the reply 
poster look bad, not the original poster.

Just to prove my point, I'm going to deal with the content of *your* 
message.  You claimed that Mr. Zitzelberger was wrong when he said that 
a lot of shop standards, rather than encouraging meaningful names, 
encouraged coding practices that have not been useful for 30 years.  A 
little hyperbole, maybe; but having worked in mainframes (albeit not IBM 
ones) for some time myself, I'd have to agree with his statement.

Not that these standards didn't help in the environment for which they 
were originally designed - sequencing your paragraphs with numbers, for 
example, can help you find things in a printed listing of your program. 
  But technology has changed to the point that most folks don't print 
listings of their programs any more - they use interactive development 
environments that let them hit Ctrl-F and find every occurrence of a 
data item or paragraph name within a program.

Unless you and Joe are friends, and this is some kind of public inside 
joke that others are not getting, I'd have to agree with MW and Richard 
- your post accomplished nothing.  What you do from this point is up to 
you, but you haven't done anything to support the cause of WS-prefixed 
variables or numbered paragraphs.
```
